Qing Meng Shi Pills [Dan Xi, same below]:

To resolve food accumulation and eliminate damp phlegm, its efficacy is particularly strong in windborne nitrate.

Ingredients:

  • Nan Xing (used for phlegm) – 2 liang, cut into slices
  • Bai Fan (alum powder) – 0.5 liang, soak in water for 2 days and dry in the sun
  • Ban Xia (pinellia) – 1 liang, soak in decoction, slice, and soak in soapberry water for 1 day, then dry in the sun
  • Nitrate: dissolve in radish water, filter, and let crystallize into waxy bovine gall – 0.5 liang
  • Meng Shi (montmorillonite) – 2 liang, crush
  • Yan Nitrate (niter) – 2 liang; mix and place in a small sand jar, cover with tiles, bind securely with iron wire, seal with salt mud, dry in the sun, and calcine red hot, then cool and remove
  • Huang Qin (Scutellaria) fried in ginger juice – 1 liang
  • Fu Ling (Poria) – 1 liang
  • Zhi Shi (bitter orange) fried – 1 liang

Grind the above into a powder, form into pills the size of a walnut using Shen Qu (a fermentation starter). Take 30 to 50 pills with white broth each time. For another method, add half a liang of Cang Zhu (Atractylodes) and 1 liang of Hua Shi, adjusting based on the patient's condition.

Furthermore, there’s a formula for treating earlier symptoms.[159p]

  • Half summer (Banxia) 2 taels
  • White Atractylodes (Baizhu)
  • Huangqin (Scutellaria)
  • Mengtian stone (calcined)
    Each 1 tael
  • Poria 7.5 qian
  • Weathered nitrate 2 qian
    Mix these into a powder, and take as before.

Zhonghe Pills treat damp phlegm and heat in the qi.

  • Cangzhu (Atractylodes)
  • Huangqin (Scutellaria)
  • Banxia (Half summer)
  • Xiangfu (Cyperus) in equal parts
    Mix these into a powder, and form into pills the size of a wutong seed. Take 50 to 70 pills with ginger soup.

Houpo-Dahuang Decoction [Zhongjing]
For those who cough after drinking wine, this must lead to coughing up blood, caused by prolonged drinking. If the pulse is weak, there will be fullness in the chest, indicating there is fluid accumulation. This formula addresses that.

  • Thick orange peel (Houpo) 1 foot
  • Dahuang (Rhubarb) 6 taels
  • Bitter orange (Zhishi) 4 taels[160p]
    Here are the translations of the provided Classical Chinese text:

  1. The three ingredients, ground and mixed with five sheng (a unit of volume) of water, are boiled to obtain two sheng. Divide into two doses and take warm.

  2. Another formula [Danxi, same below] for treating phlegm from alcohol: Qingdai, Lou Ren. Grind these into a powder and form into ginger and honey pills to dissolve and save the lungs.

  3. Another formula for treating obstructed phlegm: White silkworm, apricot kernel, Lou Ren, He Zi, mother-of-pearl, and five-fold seeds. Grind these into a powder, form into pills the size of a wutong seed. Take fifty pills with warm water.

  4. Phlegm-dispelling pills for treating all types of phlegm: Three qian of Wu Zhu Yu, half tael of Huang Lian, seven and a half qian of Hua Shi, and one tael each of Bai Fu Ling and Cang Zhu washed in water; grind into a powder, form into pills the size of a wutong seed. Take eighty or ninety pills with ginger soup.

  5. Another formula for treating Yin deficiency, with excessive food accumulation of phlegm: Seven qian of Chuan Xiong, one tael each of Huang Lian, Lou Ren, Bai Zhu, Shen Qu, and Mai Ya, half a tael of Qingdai, and three qian of Ren Zhong Bai. Grind these into a powder, soak in ginger juice, and form into pill cakes.

  6. Huang Lian Phlegm-dispelling pills for treating phlegm: One and a half tael of Ban Xia, one tael of Huang Lian, washed in Wu Zhu Yu decoction for one and a half qian, twenty-four pieces of peach kernel, ground, and half a tael of Chen Pi. Grind these into a powder, form into pills the size of a large soybean. Take one hundred pills with ginger soup.[161p]
    Baiyu Pill: Treats phlegm.
    30 kernels of Ba Dou, oil removed; 3 qian each of Nan Xing, Ban Xia, Hua Shi, and Qing Fen. Grind them into a powder. Soak with the concentrated juice of Zao Jiao Ren, form into pills the size of a Wu Zi. Take 5 to 7 pills with ginger soup.

Huang Lou Pill: Treats food accumulation with phlegm obstruction and wheezing.
Equal parts of Lou Ren, Ban Xia, Shan Zha, Shen Qu (fried), and Huang Qin. Grind into a powder, mix with melon water to form pills. Take 20 to 30 pills with ginger soup and bamboo juice. Another formula for the same condition: Lou Ren, Huang Lian (each 5 qian), Ban Xia (1 liang), and process into a paste for pills. Take 50 pills with the same treatment.

Yin Tan Pill: Treats the above condition.
1 liang of Lou Ren, 2 qian of Ban Xia, and 3 qian of Bei Mu. Grind into a powder, and form into small pills, each the size of a hemp seed. Take 100 pills with ginger soup.

Qing Ge Hua Tan Pill: Treats heat phlegm.
1 liang each of Huang Lian and Huang Qin; 5 qian each of Huang Bo and Shan Zhi; 1.5 liang of Xiang Fu; 2 liang of Cang Zhu. Grind into a powder, form into pills, and take with clear soup.

Zhui Tan Pill: Treats phlegm and fluid.[162p]
Prescription for a dark and ugly head with a two liang weight:

  • Dried bitter orange fruit (zhishi) - 1.5 liang
  • Dried bitter orange peel (zhike) - 1.5 liang
  • White alum - 0.3 liang, half fresh, half dried
  • (Stone-salt) nitrate - powdered, wind-dried
  • Pig-tooth soap pod wine, roasted - 0.2 liang each

Mix these into a powder, and use radish juice to form pills. Take fifty pills each time at dawn. Initially, there will be feces, followed by phlegm.

Another prescription for treating damp phlegm:

  • Dried cang zhu soaked - 0.3 liang
  • Roasted white atractylodes - 0.6 liang
  • Fragrant cyperus - 0.15 liang
  • White peony soaked in wine and roasted - 0.25 liang

Mix these into a powder and form into steamed buns.

Yet another prescription for treating damp phlegm in obese individuals:

  • Bitter leaf (kushen) - 0.5 liang
  • Pinellia tuber (banxia) - 0.5 liang
  • Roasted white atractylodes - 0.25 liang
  • Aged tangerine peel (chenpi) - 0.1 liang

Crush these into powder, add three slices of ginger, half a cup of bamboo fluid, and boil with water. Take far from meals, swallowing fifteen pills.

Another prescription for dispelling wind-phlegm and moving turbid qi:

  • Red-footed centipede and yellow-footed centipede - one each
  • Alum - 1 liang
  • Soap pod - 1 liang
  • Szechuan lovage (chuanxiong) - 1 liang
  • Curcuma (yujin) - 1 liang
  • Fangfeng - 2 liang

Mix into a powder, form into large pills the size of a wulzi nut, and take thirty pills before meals with tea soup. In spring, use banana stem soup to induce vomiting of phlegm.

Another prescription for treating upper-jiao wind-phlegm:

  • (Cao) Luoren - equal parts
  • Huanglian - equal parts
  • Banxia - equal parts
  • Soap pod - equal parts

Soak in ginger juice and form into steamed buns.

Another prescription for treating phlegm qi:

  • Fried baical skullcap (pianqin) - 0.25 liang
  • Pinellia tuber (banxia) - 0.25 liang
  • Roasted white atractylodes - 1 liang
  • White peony - 1 liang
  • Poria (fuling) - 0.3 liang
  • Aged tangerine peel - 0.3 liang

Mix into a powder, soak steamed buns in ginger juice, and take as pills.[163p]
Li Ge Hua Tan Wan: Treats phlegm caused by stagnation.
Ingredients:

  • Tian Nan Xing (Rhizoma Arisaematis)
  • Hā Fěn (Mussel Powder)
  • (Cāo Zé) Lou Ren (Pericarpium Trichosanthis)
  • Bei Mu (Fritillaria) with heart removed
  • Xiang Fu (Cyperus) half a tael
  • Tong Bian (Boys’ Urine)
    Make into a fine powder. Use 14 pig's tooth soap pods, break them up, add one and a half bowls of water, boil with one tael of peeled apricot kernels until the water is nearly gone, remove the soap pods, grind the apricot kernels into a paste, mix with the previous powder, add ginger juice, steam into pills the size of mung beans, and coat with Qingdai (Indigo). Take 50 pills with ginger soup.

Qing Tan Wan: Specially clears heat phlegm accumulation from the middle jiao.
Ingredients:

  • Wu Mei (Mume Fruit)
  • Ku Fan (Alum)
  • Huang Qin (Scutellaria)
  • Cang Zhu (Atractylodes)
  • Chen Pi (Dried Tangerine Peel)
  • Hua Shi (Talc) roasted
  • Qing Pi (Green Tangerine Peel)
  • Zhi Shi (Bitter Orange) each half a tael
  • Nan Xing (Tian Nan Xing)
  • Ban Xia (Pinellia)
  • Shen Qu (Fermented Dough) roasted
  • Shan Zha (Hawthorn)
  • Gan Sheng Jiang (Dried Ginger)
  • Xiang Fu (Cyperus) each one tael
    Make into a powder, soak with water, and form into pills.

Xiao Ban Xia Tang (from "Qian Jin Fang"): Treats cold in the heart and abdomen, rising phlegm affecting the chest, fullness in the sides preventing food intake, vomiting, and nausea.
Ingredients:

  • Ban Xia (Pinellia) one sheng
  • Sheng Jiang (Fresh Ginger) one jin
  • Ju Pi (Tangerine Peel) four taels
    Macerate the three ingredients and boil in ten dou of water to yield three sheng, divide into three doses. If there is urgent pain in the heart, add four taels of Gui Xin (Cinnamon Heart); if there is fullness and pain in the abdomen, add three taels of Dang Gui (Angelica). Particularly suitable for the weak and elderly. If there is no tangerine peel, replace it with three taels of Gui Xin and one tael of Gan Cao (Licorice), treatment remains the same.[164p]
    Xuanfu Flower Decoction: Treats phlegm accumulation in the chest and diaphragm, with saliva resembling glue that does not allow food to pass. Ingredients: Xuanfu flower, Xixin, Qianhu, Glycyrrhiza, and Poria each two taels; Shengjiang eight taels; Guixin four taels; Banxia one sheng; Wutou five pieces. Grind these nine ingredients, add nine sheng of water, and boil down to three sheng. Strain and divide into three doses.

Glycyrrhiza Decoction: Treats phlegm and fluid below the heart, fullness in the chest and ribs, and dizziness. Ingredients: Glycyrrhiza two taels; Guixin and Baizhu each one tael; Poria four taels. Grind these four ingredients, soak in six sheng of water, boil down to three sheng, strain, and take one sheng, three times a day. Urination should be efficient.

Mufangji Decoction: For those with fluid between the diaphragm presenting with cough and fullness, hard and firm sensation below the heart, a dark complexion, and a pulse that is deep and tight, lasting several days, with no improvement from vomiting or purging. Ingredients: Mufangji three taels; Guixin two taels; Gypsum twelve pieces, the size of a chicken egg; Ren Shen four taels. Grind these four ingredients, add six sheng of water, and boil down to two sheng, dividing into two doses. If the patient is weak, they will recover; if strong, symptoms may recur after three days, and the treatment should be repeated. If there is no improvement, remove gypsum and add Poria four taels and Mangxiao three he, using six sheng of water to prepare two sheng, after straining and dissolving the salt, dividing into two doses, mild purging indicates recovery. There is one variation that does not include Poria.[165p]
Gansui Bansha Decoction: For those with a latent pulse and a desire to relieve, who feel better after relief but still have a firm fullness in the abdomen, this is due to retained fluid wanting to depart. This formula is indicated. Use three large pieces of Gansui, twelve pieces of Bansha, one liter of water, and boil to obtain half a liter. Add five pieces of Shaoyao and one piece of Licorice the size of a finger, with another liter of water, and boil to obtain half a liter. Combine the two medicines with half a liter of honey to make a total of one and a half liters, then decoct to obtain eight doses to take at once.

Dahuoling Decoction: This treats phlegm accumulation in the chest, with fluid blockage at the navel, a rapid and full pulse, and inability to eat. It is also for wind-wood symptoms. Use four taels of Xixin, three taels each of Fuling and Baizhu, two taels each of Danggui, Jujube peel, and Fuzi, and four taels each of Shengjiang, Bansha, and Guixin. These nine ingredients should be ground and mixed with ten liters of water, boiled to obtain three liters, strained, and divided into three doses. Two or three doses are effective. One version omits Xixin and includes Ren Shen.

Fuling Decoction: Treats fullness in the chest cavity due to phlegm. Use four taels of Fuling, one sheng of Bansha, one jin of Shengjiang, and eight taels of Guixin. These four ingredients should be ground and mixed with eight liters of water, boiled to obtain two and a half liters, and divided into four doses. If the cold is severe, add four taels of Fuzi. If there is fullness of qi, add three to seven pieces of Betel nut.[166p]
Da Ban Xia Decoction: Treats phlegm, cold accumulation, and tightness in the chest.
Ingredients: Ban Xia (1 sheng), Bai Zhu (3 liang), Sheng Jiang (8 liang), Fu Ling, Ren Shen, Gui Xin, Gan Cao, Fu Zi (each 3 liang).
Method: Crush the eight ingredients, add 8 sheng of water, boil to obtain 3 sheng, and divide into two doses.

Ban Xia Decoction: Treats phlegm and cold, regurgitation, and sourness.
Ingredients: Ban Xia, Wu Zhu Yu (each 3 liang), Sheng Jiang (6 liang), Fu Zi (1 piece).
Method: Combine the four ingredients with 5 sheng of water, boil to obtain 2.5 sheng, and divide into three doses, suitable for young and old, three times a day.

Jujube Decoction: Treats swelling and fullness due to phlegm accumulation.
Ingredients: 10 large jujubes (deseeded), Da Jie, Da Huang, Huang Qin, Gan Cao, Gan Sui (each 2 liang), Yuan Hua, Rao Hua (each 5 qian).
Method: Crush the eight ingredients, add 5 sheng of water, boil to obtain 1 sheng, and divide into four doses, to be taken on an empty stomach for a swift effect.

Angelica Decoction: Treats lingering phlegm, food stagnation, and abdominal distension.
Ingredients: Dang Gui, Ren Shen, Gui Xin, Huang Qin, Gan Cao, Shao Yao (each 2 liang), Da Huang (4 liang), Sheng Jiang, Ze Xie (each 3 liang).
Method: Crush the ten ingredients, add 10 dou of water, boil to obtain 3 sheng, and divide into three doses.
Also a formula: Treats phlegm, food stagnation, and dry retching.[167p]
Ginseng, Xuanfu flower, Orange peel, and Asarum, each one tael; ZeXie, BaiZhu, Apricot kernel, and ZhiShih, each two taels; BaiFuLing, ChaiHu, Fresh ginger, BanXia, and ShaoYao, each three taels. The thirteen ingredients, crushed, should be boiled with nine sheng of water to yield two sheng and seven ge, divided into three doses, taken three times a day.

Another formula for treating phlegm and fluid accumulation in the chest, water sounds in the intestines, indigestion, and vomiting water: areca nut, twelve pieces; fresh ginger, BaiZhu, and Apricot kernel, each four taels; BanXia, eight taels; FuLing, five taels; Orange peel, three taels. The seven ingredients, crushed, should be boiled with ten dou of water to yield three sheng, strain and divide into three doses.

Ginger Pepper Decoction treats accumulated phlegm in the chest, reduced appetite, weakness of stomach qi, coughing, and vomiting. Use seven ge of ginger juice, three ge of Sichuan pepper, one tael each of cinnamon heart, Aconite, and licorice; two taels each of orange peel, Platycodon root, and FuLing; three taels of BanXia. The nine ingredients, crushed, should be boiled with nine sheng of water to yield two and a half sheng, strain, then add ginger juice boiled to yield two sheng, divided into three doses. Take three doses of this. If you wish to take large dispersing Five Stone Pills, you must first take this decoction and then take HuángQí Pills for best results. One formula does not use licorice.

Ginger Aconite Decoction treats cold phlegm, chest fullness, shortness of breath, vomiting, and headache from fluid accumulation, and indigestion. Use eight taels of fresh ginger and four taels of Aconite, fresh and cut into four pieces.[168p]
The two ingredients on the right, finely ground, are to be boiled in eight sheng of water until two sheng remain, and then divided into four doses.

The "Liao Ge San" is used to treat phlegm accumulation in the heart, along with drinking buildup, and cold-induced heart discomfort. The ingredients include twenty-eight gourd seeds, twenty-seven red beans, one portion each of ginseng and licorice. The four ingredients on the right are used for lower gastrointestinal issues; take a spoonful with wine daily. It also treats jaundice.

The "Duan Ge Tang" treats phlegm accumulation in the chest. It consists of three taels of Hengshan, one tael each of licorice and pine lichen, and twenty-three gourd tops. The four ingredients are to be ground, boiled in one and a half sheng each of water and wine until one and a half sheng remain, divided into three doses, reducing the dose gradually afterward. After achieving a satisfactory emesis, one should take Banxia decoction.

The "Song Luo Tang" is used to eliminate phlegm accumulation and heat in the chest. It includes two taels of pine lichen, fourteen each of black plum and fructus gardeniae, and three taels each of licorice and Hengshan. The five ingredients are to be ground, soaked in three sheng of wine overnight, then in the morning boiled in three sheng of water until one and a half sheng remains, strained, and taken in a single dose or divided into two doses. If one achieves satisfactory emesis, stop.

The "Du Heng Tang" is meant to induce vomiting for a hundred ailments. It contains three taels each of Du Heng and pine lichen, and thirty-seven gourd seeds. The three ingredients are to be ground, soaked in one and two-tenth sheng of wine for two nights, strained, and divided into two doses. If one vomits after one dose, stop. If vomiting does not occur, one can take another sheng if feeling as if one has traveled for a long distance, until the medicinal effect is used up. Continuous vomiting requires a thin porridge to settle the stomach. This is suitable for both young and old, and is effective.[169p]
Scallion White Soup: Treats cold and hot phlegm, headaches and disturbances during onset, with a desire to vomit but unable. Ingredients:

  • Two and seven scallion white stems
  • A handful of peach leaves
  • Ground pearl
  • Mount Heng
  • Aconite
  • Licorice, each five qian (a unit of weight)

These six ingredients are to be crushed together. Use four sheng (a volume measure) of water and wine, boil to extract three sheng, remove the dregs, and add the pearl powder. Take one sheng each time; stop once vomiting begins. If peach leaves are unavailable, use loquat leaves instead.

Xuanfu Flower Pill: Treats stagnant phlegm and dampness, obstruction in the sides, abdominal distension, weakness and inability to eat, undigested food, tendency to vomit and dry heave, irregular bowel movements either constipation or diarrhea, abdominal gurgling like water, internal heat and dry mouth with a desire to drink, sudden dizziness and fainting, pain under the ribs. Ingredients:

  • Five liang (a unit of weight) each of Xuanfu flower, cinnamon heart, bitter orange fruit, and ginseng
  • Four fen (a smaller unit of weight) each of evodia fruit, asarum, rhubarb, scutellaria, tangshen, magnolia bark, ideal flowering plant, and tangerine peel
  • Six fen each of dried ginger, peony, and white atractylodes
  • Eight fen each of poria, bite (possibly referring to a type of toxic plant), aconite, and rock salt
  • Three fen of licorice

Grind these twenty ingredients into a powder, form into pills the size of a walnut. Take five pills with wine twice daily. Adjust subsequent doses as needed. The "Extend Life Formula" excludes white atractylodes, bite, aconite, rock salt, asarum, scutellaria, magnolia bark, evodia, ideal flowering plant, tangerine peel, and licorice, instead using two fen each of soap pod and prepared aconite, three liang each of Sichuan pepper, defensive sunflower, and apricot kernel, and four fen of dried rehmannia.[170p]
Zhōngjūn hēi hóuwán: Treats accumulation of fluid and stasis, causing fullness and dimness of the eyes. Ingredients: 2 liang of yánhuā, 8 fen of bādòu, 5 fen of xìng rén, 4 fen each of guì xīn and jú gēng. Mix the five ingredients into a powder, form into honey pills the size of a broad bean. Take three pills each time, and increase as necessary.

Shùnlíu zǐ wán: Treats accumulations in the heart and abdomen, fullness in the sides, retention of fluid and phlegm, difficulty in urination and defecation, sharp pain in the lower abdomen, and obstruction above the diaphragm. Ingredients: 5 fen of shígāo, 3 fen each of dàizhǐshí, qīng shā gǔ, and bànxià, 4 fen of guì xīn, and 7 pieces of bādòu. Mix these six ingredients into a powder, form into honey pills the size of a broad bean, take one pill in the morning, increasing up to two.

Another prescription: Treats retention of phlegm and fluid, accumulation in the sides, fullness and emaciation of the abdomen, inability to eat or drink, indigestion, preference for spitting with dry heaving, and irregular bowel movements. Ingredients: 8 fen each of xuánfùhuā, dàhuáng, fùzǐ, fú lǐng, jiāomù, guì xīn, yánhuā, lángdù, gānjiāng, sháoyào, and zhǐshí, and 8 fen of xì xīn. Mix these twelve ingredients into a powder, form into honey pills the size of a walnut. Take three pills, three times a day, increasing gradually thereafter.[171p]
Left Return Decoction

This is a powerful tonic for water. In cases where phlegm illness is linked to the decline of yin at the life gate and excess of yang, this formula should be adjusted and utilized. This is the main formula for both the "One Yin Decoction" and the "Four Yin Decoction."

  • Prepared Rehmannia: 2-3 qian or up to 1-2 liang
  • Chinese Yam: 2 qian
  • Goji Berries: 2 qian
  • Cornus (Shan Yu): 1-2 qian (use less if sensitive to sour)
  • Poria: 1.5 qian
  • Honey-fried Licorice: 1 qian

Add 2 sheng of water, simmer until reduced to 7 parts, and take far from meals.

Left Return Pill

Used to treat insufficiency of true yin and kidney water, which fails to nourish the essence and defense, leading to gradual weakness and excessive phlegm. This tonic is to strengthen the water and nourish the original yin of the left kidney, allowing essence and blood to be replenished. This formula should be used.

  • Cow Knee: 3 liang, washed and steamed in wine (do not use if very smooth)
  • Prepared Rehmannia: 8 liang
  • Chinese Yam: fried
  • Goji Berries
  • Cornus (Shan Yu) flesh
  • Turtle gelatin: fried pearls
  • Cuscuta: prepared
  • Deer horn gelatin: crushed and fried pearls, each 4 liang

First, steam the prepared Rehmannia until soft, then add the paste and honey for forming into pills the size of a tung seed. Before meals, take over a hundred pills with hot soup or light salt water.[172p]
Right Return Pills: Treats the decline of the fire at the Gate of Life, which fails to generate earth, leading to phlegm and fluid accumulation. It is necessary to enhance the original fire to nourish the source yang of the right kidney; this formula addresses that.

Ingredients:

  • 8 taels of cooked Rehmannia
  • 4 taels each of stir-fried Dioscorea (Chinese yam), slightly stir-fried Goji berries, processed Cuscuta seeds, stir-fried deer antler glue, and stir-fried Eucommia bark (with ginger juice)
  • 3 taels each of slightly stir-fried Schisandra fruit and Angelica (don’t use if there is diarrhea)
  • 2 taels of prepared Aconite, gradually increased to 5 or 6 taels
  • 2 taels of cassia, gradually increased to 4 taels

Preparation: First, pound the prepared Rehmannia into a paste and mix with honey to form pills the size of a marble. Chew and take 2 or 3 pills, washed down with hot white broth, or add ginseng for quicker effects.

Liu'an Decoction: Treats symptoms not caused by initial wind invasion, phlegm stagnation, and reversed qi.

Ingredients:

  • 1.5 qian of dried tangerine peel
  • 2 to 3 qian of pinellia
  • Skinless apricot kernels
  • 1 qian each of licorice and white mustard seed (not for the young or those with weak qi)
  • 2 qian of poria

Preparation: Add 1.5 bowls of water and 3 to 7 slices of fresh ginger, simmer until reduced to 7 tenths. Take on an empty stomach.[173p]
Jin Shui Liu Jun Jian: This formula treats lung and kidney deficiency with cold symptoms, water transforming into phlegm, or cases of aging with yin deficiency, insufficient blood and qi, exposure to wind-cold, cough with nausea and vomiting, excessive phlegm, and urgent wheezing; it is remarkably effective. Ingredients: Angelica (Dang Gui) 2 qian, Pinellia (Ban Xia) 2 qian, Poria (Fu Ling) 2 qian, Cooked Rehmannia (Shu Di) 3-5 qian, Aged Tangerine Peel (Chen Pi) 1.5 qian, Honey-fried Licorice (Zhi Gan Cao) 1 qian. Use 2 sheng of water, add fresh ginger 3-5-7 slices, decoct until reduced by 70-80%, and take warm after meals.

Yi Yin Jian: This formula is used to treat water deficiency and excessive fire, hence the name "One Yin." It is suitable for cases of true kidney water deficiency with many signs of yang deficiency heat, as well as yin deficiency phlegm-heat symptoms. Ingredients: Fresh Rehmannia (Sheng Di) 2 qian, Peony (Shao Yao) 2 qian, Ophiopogon (Mai Dong) 2 qian, Salvia (Dan Shen) 2 qian, Licorice (Gan Cao) 1 qian, Cooked Rehmannia (Shu Di) 3-5 qian, Achyranthes (Niu Xi) 1.5 qian. Use 2 sheng of water and decoct until reduced by 70%, and take warm after meals.

He Wei Er Chen Jian: This formula treats stomach cold leading to phlegm, nausea, vomiting, chest fullness, and bloating. Ingredients: Dried Ginger (Gan Jiang) 1-2 qian (stir-fried), Amomum (Sha Ren) 0.4-0.5 qian, Aged Tangerine Peel (Chen Pi) 1.5 qian, Pinellia (Ban Xia) 1.5 qian, Poria (Fu Ling) 1.5 qian, Licorice (Zhi Gan Cao) 0.7 qian. Use 1.5 sheng of water, decoct until reduced by 70%, and take warm at any time.

Ling Zhu Er Chen Jian: This formula treats phlegm and water retention beneath the heart, vomiting, and acid regurgitation. Ingredients: Polyporus (Zhu Ling) 1.5 qian, Alisma (Ze Xie) 1.5 qian, Poria (Fu Ling) 1.5 qian, Pinellia (Ban Xia) 2-3 qian, Dried Ginger (Gan Jiang) 1-2 qian (stir-fried), White Atractylodes (Bai Zhu) 1-2 qian, Honey-fried Licorice (Zhi Gan Cao) 0.8 qian, Aged Tangerine Peel (Chen Pi) 1 qian. Use 1.5 sheng of water, decoct for consumption.[174p]
Divine Aroma Powder: Treats phlegm obstruction in the diaphragm; for those for whom other medicines are ineffective. Use equal parts of clove, white cardamom, or sand ginger. Grind into powder and mix with clear broth, taking five to seven parts, and for severe cases, one cash. There are no restrictions on daily use.

Chai Chen Decoction: Treats wind-cold, cough with fever, fullness, excessive phlegm, and vomiting. Ingredients: 2-3 qian of Chai Hu, 1.5 qian of Chen Pi, 1 qian of Gan Cao, 2 qian each of Ban Xia and Fu Ling, 3-5-7 slices of fresh ginger. Boil with 1.5 zhong of water until reduced by 7 parts, take warm far from meals.

Chou Xin Decoction: Treats various phlegm diseases, especially with excess fire that is not suitable for tonics. Ingredients: 1-2 qian each of Huang Qin, Shi Hu, Mu Tong, and roasted Zhi Zi, 0.5 qian each of Zhi Ke and Ze Xie, 3 fen of fine Gan Cao. Boil with 1 zhong of water until reduced by 7 parts, take warm far from meals. For severe internal heat, it is better to take it cold.

Qing Ge Decoction: Treats phlegm caused by fire movement, qi stagnation, breathlessness, fullness, and internal heat with external thirst. Ingredients: 0.5 qian of Chen Pi, 2 qian each of Hai Shi and Mu Tong, 1-2 qian of Dan Xing, 2-3 qian of Bei Mu (slightly crushed), 5-7 fen of Bai Jie Zi. Boil with 1.5 zhong of water until reduced by 7 parts, take warm. If phlegm does not subside due to strong heat, add a small zhong of childhood urine.[175p]
Li Yin Decoction: This is a variation of the Li Zhong Tang formula, used to treat symptoms of deficiency in the spleen and stomach, including phlegm and fluid.

  • Cooked Rehmannia 3-5-7 qian or 1-2 qian
  • Honey-fried Licorice 1-2 qian
  • Angelica 2-3 qian or 5-7 qian
  • Dried Ginger, toasted to yellow, 1-2-3 qian or can add Cinnamon 1-2 qian

Use 2 sheng of water, decoct until 7-8 parts remain, and consume warm. If you add Aconite, it is called Aconite Li Yin Decoction. If you add Ginseng, it is called Liu Wei Huan Yang Decoction, suitable for cases of diminished fire in the life gate with no yang in the yin.

Warm Stomach Decoction: Used for middle cold, vomiting phlegm and water, and lack of desire to eat.

  • Ginseng 1-2-3 qian or 1 liang
  • White Atractylodes, toasted, 1-2 qian or 1 liang
  • Aged Citrus Peel
  • Honey-fried Licorice each 1 qian
  • Angelica 1-2 qian; do not use if diarrhea
  • Dried Ginger, scorched, 1-2 qian
  • Hyacinth Bean 2 qian, toasted

Use 2 sheng of water, decoct to 7 parts, and consume warm away from meals. For phlegm and chest or abdominal distension, add Poria 1-2 qian.

Liu Wei Yi Gong Decoction: Used for similar conditions as above with slight stagnation.

  • Ginseng 2-3 qian
  • White Atractylodes, Poria each 2 qian
  • Honey-fried Licorice, Aged Citrus Peel each 1 qian
  • Dried Ginger, toasted to yellow, 1-2 qian

Use 1.5 sheng of water and decoct for consumption.

Holy Atractylodes Decoction: Used for phlegm and fluid vomiting.

  • Winter Atractylodes, toasted, 5-6 qian to 1-2 liang
  • Dried Ginger, toasted
  • Cinnamon each 1-2 qian

Use 1.5 sheng of water, decoct to 7 parts, and consume warm. Addition of Aged Citrus Peel is also acceptable.[176p]
Hewei Erchen Decoction: Treats coldness in the stomach leading to phlegm, nausea, vomiting, and chest fullness with belching.

Ingredients:

  • Dried ginger (stir-fried), 1-2 qian
  • Amomum, 4-5 fen
  • Aged tangerine peel, 1.5 qian
  • Pinellia, 1.5 qian
  • Poria, 1.5 qian
  • Licorice (honey-fried), 7 fen

Preparation: Use 1.5 sheng of water, decoct to 7 parts remaining, and take warm at any time.

Single Formula

For stubborn phlegm that won’t dissolve:

  • Blue stone, 1 liang; green stone, 0.5 liang, processed with water; roll into balls the size of mung beans. Take 10 balls each time, swallow with warm water, and it will expel one or two bowls of phlegm without harming the person.

There will be a subtle sound in the heart, and the chest obstruction will disperse. It does not disturb the internal organs or harm true qi. Alum transforms phlegm, while vermilion breaks down accumulation, hence it works.

Accumulating phlegm and saliva, lodged between the chest and diaphragm, causes persistent pain in the heart and abdomen, both day and night, and may be accompanied by dry retching and regurgitation. To treat this, take one tael of clam powder and fry it with seven pieces of areca nut until red, discard the nuts, and mix the powder with vinegar to form pills the size of Wutong seeds. Take twenty pills with ginger wine. For men with abdominal pain, use fennel soup. For women with pain from blood and qi, take with children's urine and wine.

When there is stagnation of qi in the chest and diaphragm leading to discomfort, phlegm and fluids are not flowing properly, and the head feels unclear: take fresh southern star anise, remove the skin, and soak it in banxia decoction for seven times, using five taels of each to make a powder. Mix with ginger juice to form a cake, cover it with paper mulberry leaves, let it turn yellow, dry it, and it should be prepared within five to six months. Wind-phlegm causing obstruction, constipation: use one large dung beetle or a pair of small ones, dry them on a new tile, grind to a powder, and mix with good wine to ingest. The dung beetle, being a manure insect, is used because of its natural ability to promote excretion and relieve blockages.

Phlegm-damp effective formula: Shells of tile clams, irrespective of quantity, are to be calcined, ground into powder. When the loquat fruit is ripe, mix with the tile powder to form cakes and dry to make powder; use honey water to adjust one qian, or incorporate it with other medicinal herbs into pills; its efficacy surpasses that of sea powder.

Acupuncture and Moxibustion

The "Gua Yi Jing" states: Overflowing fluids lead to hardness and pain below the ribs, treated at the center of the abdomen. Overflowing fluids result in blocked waterways, yellowish urine, lower abdominal pain, swelling, and diarrhea, causing body pain that radiates to the bones; the Jingmen point is key in this regard. For thirst with body heaviness and excessive salivation, the Yinbai point is essential.

The "Qianjin Fang" states: Accumulated fluids leading to fullness in the chest, indigestion of food, are treated by moxibustion at the daogu point fifty times. The "Yixue Gangmu" states: diseases caused by various phlegm include headaches, wind, cough, and all phlegm-damp issues are treated at Fenglong and Zhongwan points. Women of advanced age with symptoms of wind-phlegm show a pulse that is deep, solid, slippery, and rapid; if phlegm is below, the power is weakened; if in the middle, there is chest tightness; if above, headaches and coughs may occur, with convulsions affecting the hands and feet when dizziness strikes. The Fengmen is located two and a half cun from the skin, the Jueque is three and a third cun, the Fenglong is two and a half cun, and the Jianjing is five fen.[185p]
Guiding Principles

The "Secrets of Preserving Life" states: The method of guiding phlegm and fire involves stretching the legs while sitting firmly, using both hands to perform the Child’s Method, applying force to lift up, lowering the head and bending the body, then pulling back the toes three times. As with the method, exert force to rise, swallow saliva, and lower it into the mysterious gate, bending up twenty-four times, and then maintaining the practice afterwards.

In practicing the technique, achieve tranquility by using the sixteen-character phrase to guide the swallow at noon, focusing on the navel and keeping thoughts present. Next, practice the tailbone movements eight or nine times; entering tranquility naturally allows phlegm and fire to descend. For phlegm-related ailments, first concentrate on the area of pain, then push open the large intestine, guiding it towards the valley path to facilitate the flow of qi.

For phlegm in the chest, press both hands below the nipples and knead several times. One must focus and nurture qi, then consciously widen the chest, envisioning the upper jiao qi descending to the lower jiao, thereby allowing the diaphragm to open and phlegm to transform.

Medical Case

The "Rujun Shiqin" states: Guo Jingzhi was ill with lingering fluid for four days, resulting in edema and inability to eat; the legs were swollen and painful, connected to the kidney area. He was first treated with a bitter medication to induce vomiting, and later with boat and vehicle pills, as well as Chuan San to purge, resulting in a quick recovery.[186p]
In Zhangfeng Village by Tangxi, there was an elderly farmer named Yang who had been suffering from vomiting sour water for more than ten years. Initially, he relied on traditional drinks for relief, but various doctors treated him with drying agents, applied moxibustion and needles to his stomach and navel, yet the sores never healed. A healer used bitter medicine to aid him, and as he spat out thick phlegm, he managed to expel two to three liters of it and laughed as he recovered.

A woman, since her youth, had cried deeply, then drank ice-cold water excessively, leading to a blockage of water at her heart and subsequently to painful discomfort. Doctors all believed it was due to cold accumulation, treating her with warming medicines and prohibiting cold foods. However, just the smell of tea would trigger her symptoms. This lasted for several years, during which she underwent moxibustion and needle treatments, leading to dozens of sores. Over ten years, her urine became reddish-yellow, her bowels constipated, her vision worsened, and she accumulated water which greatly increased, flowing to her sides. People deemed it "water constitution" or "edema disorder." They applied various rough medicines to penetrate her condition, but her appetite waned over time, and the accumulation persisted, affecting her from her sternum down to her sides and lower abdomen. Whenever she had an episode, pressing her abdomen sounded like water splashing, it became hard and sensitive, with episodes occurring five to seven times a month, sometimes so severe she felt she might die. Medicine became distasteful to her over more than twenty years until she sought the help of a healer.

Upon examining her pulse, he found it deep and slow at the cun position, indicating phlegm in her chest. He first used Gua Ti San to help her expel five to seven liters of phlegm; within a few days, more phlegm and water followed. After several more days, she expelled several more liters. In total, she had three rounds of expulsion and three rounds of sweating, all of the accumulated fluids were eliminated. With a prescription of medicines to disperse dampness and resolve edema, she improved significantly within a month.[187p]
A woman had a hard mass above her navel, as firm as a fist, and pressing on it felt like stone. Everyone thought it was a case of a problematic pregnancy and sought countless remedies, including acupuncture, poisonous drugs, and prayers, all to no avail. One day, a man named Dai examined her and said: "This is cold phlegm." He checked her pulse and found it deep; surely it was cold phlegm. He used a gourd seed powder to induce vomiting, and she expelled six to seven liters, reducing the mass by more than half. After a few days, she vomited again, producing about two to three liters of frothy, foul-smelling bile resembling chicken yolk. This was repeated three times, after which he adjusted her treatment with ginseng and Wuling powder, and her abdomen returned to normal.

Zhongming's son had suffered from wind phlegm disease since the age of four, which worsened by fifteen, causing attacks once or twice a month. Each time, he experienced severe headaches, and only after punching out hundreds of times would he expel a cup of yellow-green phlegm. As the years went on, his episodes became more frequent; he saw black spots and would become disoriented for three to four days before recovering. Various doctors prescribed southern star and pinellia herbs to resolve the phlegm, but none were effective. By chance, he met a man named Dai in the southern countryside by the river. Dai used a method to induce sweating with a double solution, followed by a bitter agent to expel phlegm, and the illness improved by eight to nine parts; he continued with the adjusted doses from spring to autumn, and after several treatments, he was completely cured.[188p]
Zhuo County's Zhou Jing was returning to Luyi from the capital when he became thirsty. He drank too much water and gradually developed swelling. Some suggested using Sanhua Shenyou Pills, but he feared their potency; others recommended Wuling Powder, but it was too mild and ineffective. After several months without results, his limbs and kidneys swelled, and he became constipated. 常仲明 sought treatment from Master Dai, who instructed him to take medicine, but by the time he arrived, he had already passed away. Master Dai said: "In patients with water retention, the condition resembles a flood on a long river—using cups and spoons to remove it is nearly impossible. One must use the methods of the divine Yu to control the water for recovery."

The "Danxi Heart Method" states: "Lu An, over seventy years old, had phlegm that was not resolved in his diaphragm, and he had little appetite. Initially, it was not serious, but upon consulting doctors in Jingkou, he was prescribed Yue Ju Pills and Qing Qi Hua Tan Pills. His chest felt slightly more at ease, and he continued to take them daily without stopping for over a year, becoming increasingly worn out. When he rented a boat to come for my consultation, his flesh had greatly wasted away, and his pulse felt like floating silk, with the Tai Xi pulse completely absent. Seeing my troubled expression, he said: 'I too believe I will inevitably die, but the bloating in my diaphragm is unbearable, and my constipation is severe. If I could just feel relief for a moment, I would die with my eyes closed.' Pressured by his dire state, I prescribed a strong formula. Despite the emotional struggle of being close relatives, I advised using a large dose of ginseng and white atractylodes decoction. Shortly thereafter, he went to the toilet and expelled several liters of accumulated phlegm, feeling relief and comfort in his chest. However, a few days later, he passed away."[189p]
The "Orthodox Transmission of Medicine" states: My niece-in-law, Mrs. He, while at home, in April, due to excessive eating of green plums, developed a phlegm and fluid illness. During the day, she experienced intense pain in the chest and diaphragm, as if pierced by knives, which ceased by evening; however, her knees were in severe pain, likely due to the phlegm and fluid rising and falling with respiration. One physician diagnosed it as a cold stomach condition and treated it with dried ginger, ginger, cinnamon, aconite, rhizome, and other herbs, along with a pepper porridge, which worsened her condition, increasing her thirst and causing frequent urination.

Seeking my treatment, I diagnosed her pulse as strong, rapid, and slippery. I formulated a treatment to clear phlegm and instructed her to cook bamboo sap urgently. After three days, her thirst diminished, and her urination improved, but the pain in her chest and knees persisted as before. I gave her half a bowl of juice from radish seeds, resulting in the expulsion of about half a liter of phlegm.

That night, the pain intensified, reflecting the unrest mentioned by Danxi about the rebellious nature of the illness. The next day, I administered one ounce of ginseng and prepared a decoction with reverse flowing water, which did not induce vomiting. The following day, I provided a decoction of bitter fleece-flower root, which also failed to induce vomiting, and then gave her a combination of aconite and platycodon radix, which still did not cause vomiting. One morning, I used one qian of processed villous green plant and added a small amount of musk, mixing it with sour juice for her to take, resulting in a significant vomiting episode. By the next morning, her vomiting stabilized. Over this time, she expelled a small bucket's worth of stubborn phlegm and thick fluid, and her pain was alleviated. Subsequently, I used soft porridge to treat and soothe her.[190p]
The "Medical Essentials" states: Liu Yuanwai, who lived in colorful silk, suffered from typhoid for six or seven days, unable to sleep day and night. Whenever he managed to sleep, he would quickly wake up, and as soon as he got up, he would fall down, never having a moment's rest. He felt tired of this state and called for his servant, who said: "If this is due to qi, there should be panting; but now there are no such symptoms, so it is not qi." At that time, cold sweat appeared on his body, his pulse was deep in all positions, and the pulse at the Guān point was also deep. Upon further examination, the beating under the fingers revealed that phlegm was accumulated and cold had gathered in his chest. Thus, I used three ingredients: one ounce each of dried tangerine peel, pinellia, and dried ginger. I chopped them up, crushed half an ounce of ginger, added two bowls of water, and decocted it until there was one and a half bowls remaining, then strained it and divided it into two doses. After taking the medicine for a while, he finally slept, and did not wake for an entire day and night. When he regained consciousness, he expelled a lump of phlegm about the size of an egg, and thereafter the phlegm was resolved. Generally, in cases of phlegm, cold sweating is always present; this is its proof.[191p]
The "Essentials of Traditional Medicine" states: The head of the Ministry of Justice, Xu Ling, was overworked and angry, causing his spirit to become dull and fatigued, sweating profusely as if bathed, with incoherent speech, in a state of extreme danger. I was called to treat him. Upon examining his pulse, it was large, slippery, and soft, indicating a deficiency of qi accompanied by phlegm. I prescribed a dose of the Bu Zhong Yi Qi Decoction, adding four packets of ingredients; using one tael of ginseng, one qian of prepared aconite, and three qian of prepared pinellia. After four days, he showed slight improvement; I then used the Six Gentleman Decoction with ginger juice, and after taking it for several days, combined it with the Eight Flavor Pill, adjusting his treatment over two months, he recovered.

Hanlin scholar Li Ji suffered from excessive fatigue, became drunk, causing internal distress, sweating profusely with much phlegm. He took medicine to relax the diaphragm and transform phlegm, but felt more stagnant and uncomfortable. Upon examining his pulse, it was deep and thin, especially at the chi position. I told his son-in-law Yang Yuanrun: "Phlegm with a thin pulse indicates a difficulty in recovery. Moreover, the deep thin pulse at the chi position is a sign of damage to the essence; it cannot be treated according to standard practices." Yuanrun insisted on administering the remedy, and reluctantly used the Bu Zhong Yi Qi Decoction combined with pinellia and poria, which yielded minor effects. Everyone was pleased. I remarked: "The thin pulse remains unchanged, the pulse method has no foundation; the end is near." Indeed, over ten days later, he passed away.[192p]
Literature tells of Zhu Wen, who was plagued by insect-like pains throughout his body, his mouth and tongue rotten. Each morning, he would see two ghosts, who presented him with food while weeping, saying: "I am not yet thirty, and my elderly parents still live; these two ghosts torment me day and night, leaving me no chance of life. If I could summon the strength of the heavens to avoid death, I would be akin to the people of Qin and Yue today." Thus, he knocked his head until it bled. I examined his pulse, which fluctuated between strong and weak, suspecting it was due to ghostly influence. Upon closely observing, I found his two pulses slippery and large, concluding it was a illness caused by phlegm and fluid. I administered three qian of medicine for phlegm. Though it produced slight results, his condition remained unchanged; I then gave him two qian of small stomach pills, which expelled over ten bowls of phlegm and fluid, reducing his pain by half. By morning, the ghosts were gone. I subsequently boiled three qian of ginseng and two qian of white atractylodes, and after taking three qian of small stomach pills, he had a massive discharge of over ten movements, amounting to about twenty bowls, and his illness vanished. Finally, I used Six Gentleman pills, and after taking four jins, he was cured.

The "Compendium of Materia Medica" states: Hong Mai said: Mai had phlegm illness, and upon facing the night, sent a messenger with instructions: chew three walnuts and three slices of ginger while lying down, and drink two or three sips of soup; then chew the walnuts and ginger again as before, and remain quietly lying down for certain recovery. When Mai returned to Yu Tang and followed these instructions, by morning his phlegm dissipated and his cough ceased.[193p]
Li Yang's young son was afflicted with phlegm and wheezing, not nursing or eating for five days and nights, and the doctor warned that it was critical. That night, his wife dreamed of Guanyin, who gave her a decoction of ginseng and walnut. Li hurriedly obtained about an inch of ginseng from Silla and one walnut, brewed them in a clam shell's worth of water, and administered it; the wheezing immediately ceased. The next day, after removing the walnut's skin and using it again, the wheezing returned. Using it with the skin still attached, he trusted the remedy overnight and was cured. This remedy is not included in any texts. It is said that ginseng calms wheezing, and walnut with the skin can consolidate lung function.

Zhong Cung's son, Zhang Zheng, struggled with phlegm, suffering from symptoms similar to colds or changes in weather whenever he ate until full, experiencing headaches and chills, with sour vomiting every ten days. He would lie down for several days without food; medicines provided no relief. In the early years of Xuanhe, while serving in Shunchang, he obtained a recipe for a Dan from Wu, and after taking it, he never experienced those symptoms again. Whenever he overindulged in food and felt full, he would take five to seventy pills, which sufficed. Shortly after, his urination carried the scent of evodia, and all alcoholic drinks left with his urine. He tried many phlegm remedies previously, none compared to this. He soaked Wu Zhu Yu (evodia) in water seven times, ground it with poria into a powder, and mixed it with honey into walnut-sized pills; he took fifty of them with warm water. The Mei Yangqing formula only used evodia, soaked in wine for three nights, and mixed with powdered poria, dried in the sun, swallowing a hundred pills each day with warm wine.[194p]

Within Yangshan, the individual is prone to anger, experiences discomfort in the chest, produces excessive phlegm, swallows sour and putrid substances, has diminished appetite, and feels heat in the hands and feet. The "Ancient and Modern Medical Guidelines" states: A woman aged twenty, somewhat overweight and quick-tempered, after being angry, developed a cough with phlegm and nausea. She was given the Erchen Decoction with additional ingredients to soothe the qi, reduce fire, and resolve phlegm. It was as if throwing water against a stone; gradually she coughed violently, making strange noises day and night without pause. The ancients referred to “coughing reversely” as a series of fifty or seventy sounds in quick succession, or sometimes thirty or fifty. This case involved continuous sound without end, which seemed indicative of severe illness. Her pulse was floating and slightly surging, with a sinking, slightly slippery touch. I decided to give her a rolling phlegm pill, but it had no effect. I believed phlegm was stagnant between the stomach and chest, preventing the qi from flowing. Only through coughing could it be expelled, so I used a preparation of melon stem powder mixed with sour jujube water, using a goose feather to help induce vomiting, resulting in a bowlful of phlegm being expelled, and her cough ceased, silent and effective.

After two days, due to eating noodles and meat sauce, she again developed coughing and regurgitation. I gave her clear phlegm and qi-soothing medicine, but it did not work. I reasoned that the phlegm she had expelled the previous day was not fully eliminated, so she needed to vomit again. After another effort, she expelled about half a bowl of phlegm and was then cured.[206p]
A man over forty years old, stout in build, often burdened by fatigue and phlegm heat. One day, he first felt cold, then developed a fever, experiencing slight head pain, frequent dizziness, as if he could not bear the weight, and restless heat that did not subside. He tried to sweat it out with a ginseng and perilla drink, but it did not help and sought treatment. Upon examining his pulse, it was deep, surging, and slippery. The "Yuyi Cao" states: Mr. Qian Shuweng, an elderly gentleman, has a thin physique and usually has a lot of heat and little phlegm. In recent years, the heat within him has combined with humidity to produce phlegm. In the autumn of the Xin Si year, with a mixture of dampness and heat, he suddenly suffered from numbness in his right foot, feeling as cold as ice or stone. This was due to extreme heat manifesting as cold, similar to the summer months when rain and hail occur. The doctors, observing the coldness of his foot, did not discern that it was extreme heat resembling cold, mistakenly prescribed warming herbs such as cowherb, papaya, and others; they furthermore believed that his lower energy was depleted, wrongly using warming tonics like cinnamon and aconite.

By adding fire to fire and heat to heat, his condition worsened, leading to swelling and discharge of pus. Over several months, it spread down to the ankle bones, causing swelling in the back of the foot and toes, rendering them unusable. This was entirely due to mismanagement of treatment. The reasoning is quite clear and not difficult to differentiate. If it were just cold phlegm sinking down, it would merely cause hardening and not dispersing; at worst, it would lead to weakness and numbness. How could it result in swelling and pus, with yellow water oozing and decaying flesh penetrating tendons? Mr. Taiweng, unaware of the medical errors, blamed it on the deities in the corners, but could it truly be so? This is not much different from the complications of typhoid where heat invades the meridians and causes flux.

The ginseng paste used only focused on replenishing the vital energy, without any medications to clear and resolve the damp heat, which is why there was no evident resolution of the symptoms.[208p]
As a senior official, one should not be involved in trivial matters. If ginseng works in harmony with bamboo leaf, ginseng strengthens the meridians, and bamboo leaf opens the collaterals, then their sweet and cold properties would complement each other wonderfully. Master Xu Taiye took ginseng to treat his deficiency wind, but mistakenly combined it with the heat of aconite. To this day, his sinews are shortened and he cannot move freely, which is due to his lack of understanding of how sweet and cold can open the meridians. Moreover, after the old gentleman used ginseng paste, his spleen qi became robust and his digestive function improved. At this time, if he could moderate his diet, allowing the yang qi generated within the spleen to actively dispel phlegm and heat, then the phlegm heat would circulate, and the affliction in his legs could be resolved.[209p]
On the day of an eclipse, along with night eating, although the spleen's energy is robust and not harmed by food, it is indeed regrettable that the spleen's energy, generated by ginseng, is solely used to process food and not to move phlegm or heat. Nowadays, food intake is also difficult to process, which, instead of promoting health, leads to decline, causing phlegm and fluids to accumulate in the chest, resulting in fullness and discomfort, as well as frequent coughing with phlegm not being expelled. This is fundamentally due to the spleen losing its strength, and not because the stomach is able to move fluids; rather, food intake generates phlegm, gradually saturating the lung pathways, making coughing difficult.

Although treating phlegm is urgent, the medicines for phlegm generally exhaust energy and induce deficiency, thus risking that phlegm may not be expelled before pathogenic wind enters. The only proper way is to use sweet and cold medicines to quell wind, dispel heat, moisten dryness, and replenish deficiency, thereby opening phlegm. As for spicy and warming medicines, they must not be mistakenly used again. Physicians clearly see this, yet they continue to use large amounts of cinnamon and other warming agents, presumably thinking them effective because purulent fluid easily dries up, and thus they push vigorously for a resolution. Is this not deserving of condemnation?[210p]
The old man You is robust and shows vitality, never suffering from illness or invasion of pathogens. However, every morning, he must expel a few mouthfuls of phlegm after bathing his face, habitually rubbing his chest and stimulating the energy beneath his ribs. If he sleeps soundly at night and has clear pathways for water, he feels refreshed in his chest. His symptoms resemble illness but are not truly so. After extensive examination of medical texts and inquiring with knowledgeable doctors, no clear answer could be found. Chang posited that this is phlegm and fluid accumulating in the chest cavity, with a minor stagnation formed due to his vigorous energy, leading to simultaneous gathering and expulsion. Thus, the phlegm and fluid do not cause harm; however, the energy in the chest can be injured by the act of expulsion. The chest region, or “Dan Zhong,” is at the same level as the upper burner. The classics state that the upper burner is like mist, indicating that its energy is diffused like fog. It also states that Dan Zhong is the official in charge of distributing energy within the chest and transmitting it downward. Now, due to frequent expulsions, the diffused energy turns into urgency and ascends, but when settled, it resumes its downward distribution, thus not causing harm. Generally, phlegm is the symptom while energy is the root; treating the symptom is easy, but treating the root is difficult. The difficulty in treating the root lies in the fact that previous sages have never discussed methods for it, leaving later generations unaware of how to treat it. Chang attempted to theorize on this: there are three sources of energy to address: First is lung energy. When lung energy is clear, the body's energy flows smoothly downward, and the old man’s lung energy is typically clear. Second is stomach energy. When stomach energy is harmonious, the energy within the chest also flows more easily downward, and the old man’s stomach energy is usually harmonious. Third is bladder energy.[211p]
When the energy of the bladder is strong, it can attract the energy within the chest to descend. Therefore, young students should cultivate this energy, which is inherently vigorous. If the energy in the heart region is chaotic, it can be treated immediately; if it is disturbed, it can be calmed right away. This depends on the subtle transport of these three energies, so one does not perceive their burdens, and this is called being free from illness. However, when these three energies oppose the chest diaphragm, the resulting tightness and distension can hardly be tolerated! Thus, undiagnosed illnesses may remain unspoken, but the occurring energies must urgently return to their balance. The patient may feel tormented by phlegm and fluids, attempting to expel the energy through breathing, thereby dislodging phlegm from the chest diaphragm, yet unaware that this phlegm cannot be expelled and only damages the energy. Indeed, at night, phlegm accumulates in the stomach, and upon waking, it can be naturally vomited out; during the day, the stomach's fluids circulate through the organs, and even if stimulated, they will not be expelled.[212p]
Thus, when phlegm is resolved, the qi will naturally be smooth; therefore, treating phlegm should be a priority. However, when the body is full of phlegm, it is not easily eliminated, and one must first strengthen the spleen; when the spleen is strong, new phlegm does not form. As for the old phlegm that is lodged in the body's cavities, it gradually penetrates the stomach and is dispersed both above and below; thus, without phlegm there is no vomiting, without vomiting the qi remains undisturbed, and when the qi is undisturbed, it will gradually return to its harmonious state. Nevertheless, there is still a critical point that must be addressed. The human chest is vast and empty like the great void; when the earth's qi rises, it becomes clouds. The heavenly qi must descend to become rain, and the earth's qi then begins to collect and remain still. Indeed, if the upper Jiao is like mist, the middle Jiao like fermentation, and the lower Jiao like a channel, one will understand how clouds move and rain falls, and as a result, all waterways will be filled, and the paths will be cleared, bringing a new scene to the universe. This principle places vital importance on the bladder meridian.[213p]
The classics say: The bladder is the official of the capital region; it stores the fluids, and when Qi is transformed, it can be expelled. Just as a person can drink without limit and not become drunk, this is all due to the Qi transformation of the bladder. The Zang Zhong (the area of the heart) is located within the diaphragm, while the bladder is situated in the abdomen. When the Qi of the bladder transforms, it creates a hollow space that is good for storage, allowing the Qi of the Zang Zhong to descend; if the bladder does not transform, the abdomen will already be distended, making it impossible for the Qi of the Zang Zhong to descend.

However, in order for the bladder's Qi to transform, its authority lies in preserving the kidneys, as the kidneys regard the bladder as a cavity. When kidney Qi moves, it must first flow into the bladder. If the movement is excessive and persistent, the bladder becomes full and distended, inevitably causing a reverse flow into the chest and diaphragm, resulting in a blockage that cannot be easily described. If the kidney Qi is not active, then its storage becomes firmer, allowing the bladder to remain clear and unaffected, thus the Qi of the Zang Zhong cannot be overfilled.

When the Qi of the Zang Zhong flows downward swiftly, it is not disturbed by the pulls of excess, and the chest feels open as if in the vastness of space. Chang Geng further states: When Qi is flowing smoothly, phlegm does not linger; thus, without treating the phlegm, it eliminates itself. The servant asked: Phlegm in the diaphragm is not far from the throat, and it must cause pain and vomiting each morning; why is this? The answer: It is a difference in pathways. Between the chest and diaphragm lies a thick layer of fatty membranes, completely blocking any gaps; from where can the phlegm escape? What comes out is just the phlegm from the stomach. The servant asked: Then does the phlegm in the diaphragm not come out? The answer: How could it not come out? It is just that expulsion is challenging.[214p]
The Qi of the diaphragm spreads throughout the twelve meridians. When it spreads to the six Yang meridians of the hands and feet, the Qi rises and exits from the throat; when it spreads to the six Yin meridians of the hands and feet, the Qi descends and exits from the front and back Yin. However, the Qi that exits from below is unobstructed, while the Qi that exits from above must first descend to the Yangming before it can rise, which makes it more difficult. It is said that this discussion of the transformation of the bladder Qi is profoundly subtle. Yet, the mechanism of suction is not seen in the classics; could it possibly have a source? It is stated that the "Inner Canon" contains the principle of the Greater Yang drawing the essence, but due to the lack of annotations, people cannot comprehend it. The Greater Yang refers to the bladder of the Sun. This means that the bladder can draw down the Qi from the chest, resulting in the dissipation of the distension in the chest, which serves as sufficient proof.[215p]
It is said: The theory of the "cavity in the chest" is indeed certain and without doubt, but one does not know what caused it, where it ends, or when it dissipates. It is said: The Qi of the human body, when abundant, flows into the meridians; when the meridians are abundant, Qi flows into the collaterals. The origin of the cavity begins with phlegm accumulating at the stomach, and during vomiting, the stomach Qi moves several times, with half emerging from the throat and half entering the collaterals. The collaterals of the stomach pass through the diaphragm. When the Qi rushes in urgently, it penetrates the diaphragm, allowing the phlegm to settle there. Once the phlegm has been there for a long time, it obstructs the airway, and the rushing Qi forms another cavity, like a bee's nest, growing day by day, hence making treatment very difficult. One must first eliminate the phlegm in the stomach without causing vomiting or irritation, so that the Qi of the stomach does not rush into the collaterals, allowing the stomach to become more receptive to the Qi returning from the collaterals. Gradually, using medicine to guide the cavity and clear away the phlegm, healing will occur spontaneously. This understanding is unique to me and has been tested multiple times.[216p]
Huang, my esteemed brother, the lady is suffering from phlegm obstructing the passages. There is stagnation between the liver, lungs, and pericardium; there is weakness in all three pulse sections, with excess evil and deficient righteousness, making it difficult to disperse. If one wishes to use an expelling method to slightly induce a吐 (vomit) of about thirty percent, it would indeed be a relief. However, upon careful consideration, this method is hardly feasible. The expelling technique is like a military tactic, where security and danger are easily reversed; it is inherently perilous. Moreover, phlegm is merely a fleeting disturbance; if we apply expelling medicine, the phlegm may stir and be expelled just as someone is about to address it. However, the point of entry is blocked, and the qi cannot descend. The medicinal effect conflicts with the pathological condition, possibly leading to days of unconsciousness. What can be done? Ultimately, as Danxi stated: if expulsion is feared, should it not be better to nourish and guide it downward? The phlegm in the passages cannot be led downward; it merely consumes the spleen qi, causing further obstruction. This method is not advisable either. For the current plan, it is necessary to focus on nourishing the spleen first. The spleen qi is the yang energy that supports health and movement in the body, much like the sun in the sky. When the yin condenses and blocks, it is as if the sun has lost its position; when phlegm obscures perception, it signifies that the spleen has lost its authority. Nourishing the spleen is akin to the blazing sun at its zenith; can a wisp of cloud obscure it? Next, nothing is better than clearing the lungs.[217p]
The lungs are the organ of the general and commander. When the qi is clear, it will be solemn and descend; when the qi descends, phlegm will serve as a stronghold, illustrating flaws that can be targeted for attack. The saying goes, “To attack the strong, even flaws are strong; to attack flaws, the strong also have flaws.” Now, with the limbs swollen and numb, the qi is extremely stagnant, and it cannot be neglected.

To address this condition, the method concerning the spleen must combine medicine with diet. White rice, fragrant vegetables, and bitter tea are preferable. It is essential to avoid smooth and greasy foods; even porridge should not be consumed, as it tends to form phlegm easily. Not only should mixed foods be avoided, but rice should also be reduced to prevent spleen qi from being used to digest grains; instead, it should be used to eliminate phlegm, which is much more effective than medication.

Spicy, alcoholic, and sun-dried foods all harm the lungs and should be avoided. As for medication, there is a particular order to follow, waiting for gradual improvement and full recovery in the coming spring. Due to this illness, patients do not understand treatment, and the sages of old also did not have superior methods. However, after much thought, I believe the key lies here.

Therefore, those who want to sustain this must agree with the strategies of a fortified city, first eliminating the smaller issues and then eliminating the major ones, as this is not something easily achieved. It is said that the onset of phlegm originates from the liver and gallbladder; in winter, the wood qi returns to its root, preventing aggressive treatment. Thus, we only use spleen-regulating medicines. Only when spring arrives, with the wood qi flourishing, should we employ the Four Gentlemen Decoction, adding gentian, aloe, realgar, coptis, and indigo among other herbs in pill form. This will resolve the symptoms of phlegm obstruction, leading to complete recovery and preventing future occurrences.[218p]
